</table>
<p><b>John Adams</b> (October 30 [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> October 19]&#160;1735&#160;– July 4, 1826) was an <a href="/wiki/Patriot_(American_Revolution)" title="Patriot (American Revolution)">American patriot</a> who served as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">second President of the United States</a> (1797–1801) and the first <a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President</a> (1789–97).<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> He was a lawyer, diplomat, statesman, political theorist, and, as a <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Father</a>, a leader of the movement for American independence from <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a>. He was also a dedicated diarist and correspondent, particularly with his wife and closest advisor <a href="/wiki/Abigail_Adams" title="Abigail Adams">Abigail</a>.</p>
<p>John Adams collaborated with his cousin, revolutionary leader <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Adams" title="Samuel Adams">Samuel Adams</a>, but he established his own prominence prior to the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Massacre" title="Boston Massacre">Boston Massacre</a>, he provided a successful (though unpopular) legal defense of the accused British soldiers, in the face of severe local anti-British sentiment and driven by his devotion to the <a href="/wiki/Right_to_counsel" title="Right to counsel">right to counsel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Presumption_of_innocence" title="Presumption of innocence">"protect[ion] of innocence"</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-2">[2]</a></sup> Adams was a delegate from <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Massachusetts_Bay" title="Province of Massachusetts Bay">Massachusetts</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Congress" title="Continental Congress">Continental Congress</a>, where he played a leading role in persuading Congress to declare independence. He assisted <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">Thomas Jefferson</a> in drafting the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> in 1776, and was its foremost advocate in the Congress. As a diplomat in Europe, he helped negotiate the eventual <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">peace treaty</a> with <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, and acquired vital governmental loans from <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a> bankers. Adams was the primary author of the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="Massachusetts Constitution">Massachusetts Constitution</a> in 1780. This influenced the development of America's own constitution, as did his earlier <i><a href="/wiki/Thoughts_on_Government" title="Thoughts on Government">Thoughts on Government</a></i> (1776).</p>
<p>Adams's credentials as a revolutionary secured for him two terms as President <a href="/wiki/George_Washington%27s" class="mw-redirect" title="George Washington's">George Washington's</a> vice president (1789 to 1797) and also his own election in 1796 as the second president. In his single term as president, he encountered fierce criticism from the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Jeffersonian Republicans</a>, as well as the dominant faction in his own <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a>, led by his rival <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>. Adams signed the controversial <a href="/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts" title="Alien and Sedition Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</a>, and built up the army and navy in the face of an undeclared naval "<a href="/wiki/Quasi-War" title="Quasi-War">Quasi-War</a>" with France. The major accomplishment of his presidency was a peaceful resolution of the conflict in the face of Hamilton's opposition. Due to his strong posture on defense, Adams is "often called the father of the <a href="/wiki/American_Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="American Navy">American Navy</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> He was the first U.S. president to reside in the executive mansion, now known as the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a>.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1800" title="United States presidential election, 1800">1800</a>, Adams lost re-election to Thomas Jefferson and retired to Massachusetts. He eventually resumed his friendship with Jefferson upon the latter's own retirement by initiating a correspondence which lasted fourteen years. He and his wife established a family of politicians, diplomats, and historians now referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Adams_political_family" title="Adams political family">Adams political family</a>. Adams was the father of <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">sixth</a> President of the United States. He died on the fiftieth anniversary of the adoption of the Declaration of Independence, and the same day as Jefferson. Modern historians in the aggregate have <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">favorably ranked his administration</a>.</p>
<p></p>
