<p>The election of 1796 was the first contested American presidential election.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Twice, George Washington had been elected to office unanimously; however, during his <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_George_Washington" title="Presidency of George Washington">presidency</a>, deep philosophical differences between the two leading figures in the administration—Alexander Hamilton and Thomas Jefferson—regarding domestic economic policy and U.S. foreign policy caused a rift between them,<sup id="cite_ref-FerlingTime_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FerlingTime-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> and led to the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic-Republican Party (United States)">Democratic-Republican Party</a>. Thus, when Washington announced that he would not be a candidate for a third term, an intense <a href="/wiki/Partisan_(political)" title="Partisan (political)">partisan</a> struggle for control of Congress and the presidency began.</p>
<p>Like the previous two presidential elections, no candidates were put forward for voters to choose between in 1796. The Constitution provided for the selection of electors who would then elect a president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1962898.E2.80.93899_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1962898.E2.80.93899-100">[100]</a></sup> In seven states voters chose the presidential electors. In the remaining nine states, they were chosen by the state's legislature.<sup id="cite_ref-JAelections_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JAelections-101">[101]</a></sup> The clear favorite of Democratic–Republicans was Jefferson, although he was very reluctant to run.<sup id="cite_ref-McDonald178181_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald178181-102">[102]</a></sup> There was little doubt that Adams would be the choice of a great majority of the Federalists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1962898.E2.80.93899_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1962898.E2.80.93899-100">[100]</a></sup> Even so, Hamilton also had hoped to lead the party.<sup id="cite_ref-diggins8388_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diggins8388-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Democratic-Republicans in Congress held a <a href="/wiki/Congressional_nominating_caucus" title="Congressional nominating caucus">nominating caucus</a> and named Jefferson and <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a> as their presidential choices.<sup id="cite_ref-diggins8388_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diggins8388-103">[103]</a></sup> Jefferson at first declined the nomination, but he finally agreed to run a few weeks later. Federalist members of Congress held an informal nominating caucus and named Adams and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Pinckney" title="Thomas Pinckney">Thomas Pinckney</a> as their candidates for president.<sup id="cite_ref-McDonald178181_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McDonald178181-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoadley198654_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoadley198654-104">[104]</a></sup> The campaign, was, for the most part, unorganized and sporadic, confined to newspaper attacks, pamphlets and political rallies;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1962898.E2.80.93899_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1962898.E2.80.93899-100">[100]</a></sup>of the four contenders, only Burr actively campaigned.<sup id="cite_ref-JAelections_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JAelections-101">[101]</a></sup> Adams specifically stated that he wanted to stay out of what he called the "silly and wicked game" of campaigning for office.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._16_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._16-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the campaign progressed, fears grew among Hamilton and his supporters that Adams was too vain, opinionated, unpredictable and stubborn to follow their directions once elected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1993513.E2.80.9337_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1993513.E2.80.9337-106">[106]</a></sup> Desiring "a more pliant president than Adams," Hamilton maneuvered to tip the election to Pinckney. He coerced South Carolina Federalist electors, pledged to vote for "<a href="/wiki/Favorite_son" title="Favorite son">favorite son</a>" Pinckney, to scatter their second votes among candidates other than Adams. Hamilton's scheme was undone, however, when several <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> state electors heard of it, conferred, and agreed not to vote for Pinckney.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1962902_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1962902-107">[107]</a></sup> Adams was nonetheless angered, writing shortly after the election that Hamilton was a "proud Spirited, conceited, aspiring Mortal always pretending to Morality, with as debauched Morals as old Franklin who is more his Model than any one I know."<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the end, Adams won the presidency by a narrow margin, receiving 71 electoral votes to 68 for Jefferson, who became the vice president; Pinckney finished in third with 59 votes, and Burr came in fourth with 30. The balance of the Electoral College votes were dispersed among nine other candidates.<sup id="cite_ref-ECscore_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ECscore-109">[109]</a></sup> This is the only election to date in which a president and vice president were elected from opposing tickets.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Presidency,_1797–1801"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency.2C_1797.E2.80.931801">Presidency, 1797–1801</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_John_Adams" title="Presidency of John Adams">Presidency of John Adams</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PhiladelphiaPresidentsHouse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/PhiladelphiaPresidentsHouse.jpg/220px-PhiladelphiaPresidentsHouse.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/PhiladelphiaPresidentsHouse.jpg/330px-PhiladelphiaPresidentsHouse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/PhiladelphiaPresidentsHouse.jpg/440px-PhiladelphiaPresidentsHouse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="674" data-file-height="455" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PhiladelphiaPresidentsHouse.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/President%27s_House_(Philadelphia)" title="President's House (Philadelphia)">President's House</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>. Adams occupied this Philadelphia mansion from March 1797 to May 1800.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adams was <a href="/wiki/Inauguration_of_John_Adams" title="Inauguration of John Adams">sworn into office</a> as the nation's second president on March 4, 1797, by <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Ellsworth" title="Oliver Ellsworth">Oliver Ellsworth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> As president, he followed Washington's lead in using the presidency to exemplify republican values and <a href="/wiki/Civic_virtue" title="Civic virtue">civic virtue</a>; and his service was free of scandal. He continued to strengthen the central government by expanding the Navy and Army. In July 1798 Adams signed into law the <a href="/wiki/An_Act_for_the_relief_of_sick_and_disabled_seamen" title="An Act for the relief of sick and disabled seamen">Act for the Relief of Sick and Disabled Seamen</a>, which authorized the establishment of a government-operated marine hospital service.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historians debate his decision to retain en masse the members of Washington's cabinet. Many felt he was oblivious to the political danger of such a decision, in light of the cabinet's loyalty to Hamilton. The "Hamiltonians who surround him," Jefferson soon remarked, "you are only a little less hostile to him than to me."<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._16_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._16-105">[105]</a></sup> Although aware of the Hamilton factor, Adams was convinced their retention ensured a smoother succession.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001471_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001471-113">[113]</a></sup> Adams' economic programs maintained those of Hamilton, who indeed had regularly consulted with key cabinet members, especially the powerful Secretary of the Treasury, <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Wolcott_Jr." title="Oliver Wolcott Jr.">Oliver Wolcott Jr.</a><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Adams was in other respects quite independent of his cabinet, often making decisions despite strong opposition from it. Such self-reliance enabled him to avoid war with France, despite a strong desire among his cabinet secretaries for the conflict. The <a href="/wiki/Quasi-War" title="Quasi-War">Quasi-War</a> with France resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_1800" title="Convention of 1800">detachment from European affairs</a> that Washington had sought. It also had psychological benefits, allowing America to view itself as holding its own against a European power.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._16_105-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._16-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historian George Herring argues that Adams was the most independent-minded of the founders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerring200889_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerring200889-115">[115]</a></sup> Though he aligned with the Federalists, he was somewhat a party unto himself, disagreeing with the Federalists as much as he did the Democratic-Republicans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2004647_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2004647-116">[116]</a></sup> He was often described as "prickly", but his tenacity was fed by good decisions made in the face of universal opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerring200889_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerring200889-115">[115]</a></sup> Adams was often combative, which diminished presidential decorum, as Adams himself admitted in his old age: "[As president] I refused to suffer in silence. I sighed, sobbed, and groaned, and sometimes screeched and screamed. And I must confess to my shame and sorrow that I sometimes swore."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis199357_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis199357-117">[117]</a></sup> Adams' resolve to advance peace with France, rather than to continue hostilities, especially reduced his popularity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerring200890_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerring200890-118">[118]</a></sup> This played an important role in his reelection defeat, however he was so pleased with the outcome that he had it engraved on his tombstone. Adams spent much of his term at home in Massachusetts, ignoring the details of political patronage nursed by other office holders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerring200891_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerring200891-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Quasi-War_and_peace_with_France_(1798–1800)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Quasi-War_and_peace_with_France_.281798.E2.80.931800.29">Quasi-War and peace with France (1798–1800)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/XYZ_Affair" title="XYZ Affair">XYZ Affair</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quasi-War" title="Quasi-War">Quasi-War</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fries_Rebellion" class="mw-redirect" title="Fries Rebellion">Fries Rebellion</a></div>
<p>The president's term was marked by disputes concerning the country's role, if any, in the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">expanding conflict</a> in Europe, where <a href="/wiki/French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="French Revolutionary Wars">Britain and France were at war</a>. Hamilton and the Federalists supported Britain, while Jefferson and the Democratic-Republicans favored France.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The French had supported Jefferson for president and became even more belligerent at his loss.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerring200882_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerring200882-121">[121]</a></sup> When Adams entered office, he decided to continue Washington's policy of staying out of the European war. The intense battle over the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a> in 1795 had previously polarized politics throughout the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._17_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._17-122">[122]</a></sup> The French saw America as Britain's junior partner and began seizing American merchant ships that were trading with the British. Nevertheless, most Americans were initially pro-French due to France's assistance during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">Revolutionary War</a>, and would not have sufficiently rallied behind anyone to stop France.<sup id="cite_ref-k13_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k13-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> On May 16, Adams gave a speech to the House and Senate in which he called for increasing defense capabilities in case of war with France.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> For this he was roundly derided in the Republican press.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001485_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001485-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg/220px-Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg/330px-Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg/440px-Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="816" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Property_protected_%C3%A0_la_fran%C3%A7oise.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A political cartoon depicts the XYZ Affair – America is a female being plundered by Frenchmen. (1798)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sentiments changed with the <a href="/wiki/XYZ_Affair" title="XYZ Affair">XYZ Affair</a>. Adams appointed a three-member commission to represent the United States to negotiate with France. The commission consisted of <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Cotesworth_Pinckney" title="Charles Cotesworth Pinckney">Charles Cotesworth Pinckney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elbridge_Gerry" title="Elbridge Gerry">Elbridge Gerry</a>. However, when the envoys arrived in October 1797, they were kept waiting for several days, and then granted only a 15-minute meeting with French Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Charles_Maurice_de_Talleyrand-P%C3%A9rigord" title="Charles Maurice de Talleyrand-Périgord">Talleyrand</a>. After this, the diplomats were met by three of Talleyrand's agents. Each refused to conduct diplomatic negotiations unless the United States paid enormous bribes, one to Talleyrand personally, and another to the Republic of France.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001495_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001495-127">[127]</a></sup> Supposedly this was to make up for offenses given to France by Adams in his speech.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> The Americans refused to negotiate on such terms.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001495.E2.80.9396_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001495.E2.80.9396-129">[129]</a></sup> Marshall and Pinckney returned home, while Gerry remained.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001502_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001502-130">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>The affair substantially weakened popular American support of France. The pro-French Jeffersonians lost support and quickly became the minority as many began to demand full-scale war. The affair heightened fears of sedition by the administration's opponents and legislation was introduced in response. The president knew that America would be unable to win a conflict, as France at the time was dominating the fight in most of Europe. Adams therefore pursued a strategy whereby American ships harassed French ships in an effort sufficient to stem the French assaults on American interests. This was the undeclared naval war between the U.S. and France–the <a href="/wiki/Quasi-War" title="Quasi-War">Quasi-War</a> which broke out in 1798.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._17_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._17-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>There was danger of invasion from the more powerful French forces, so Adams and the Federalist congress built up the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Army" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Army">army</a>, bringing back Washington as its commander. Washington wanted Hamilton to be his second-in-command and Adams reluctantly accommodated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1993714.E2.80.9319_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElkinsMcKitrick1993714.E2.80.9319-131">[131]</a></sup> It became apparent that Hamilton was truly in charge due to Washington's advanced years.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._17_122-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._17-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Adams also rebuilt the Navy, adding <a href="/wiki/Six_original_United_States_frigates" class="mw-redirect" title="Six original United States frigates">six fast, powerful frigates</a>, most notably the <a href="/wiki/USS_Constitution" title="USS Constitution">USS <i>Constitution</i></a>. To pay for the military buildup, Congress imposed new taxes on property: the Direct Tax of 1798.<sup id="cite_ref-k13_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-k13-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> It was the first (and last) such federal tax. Taxpayers were angered, especially in southeast Pennsylvania, where the bloodless <a href="/wiki/Fries%27s_Rebellion" title="Fries's Rebellion">Fries's Rebellion</a> broke out among rural German-speaking farmers who protested what they saw as a threat to their republican liberties and to their churches.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hamilton assumed control in the War department, and the rift between Adams' and Hamilton's supporters widened. Many sought to vest Hamilton with command authority over the army, and they also resisted giving prominent Democratic-Republicans positions in the army, which Adams wanted to do in order to gain bipartisan support. By building a large <a href="/wiki/Standing_army" title="Standing army">standing army</a>, Hamilton's supporters raised popular alarms and played into the hands of the Democratic-Republicans. They also alienated Adams and his large personal following. They shortsightedly viewed the Federalist party as their own tool and ignored the need to pull together the entire nation in the face of war with France. Overall, however, patriotic sentiments and a series of naval victories, popularized the war as well as the president.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurtz1957331_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurtz1957331-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 1799, Adams surprised many by sending diplomat <a href="/wiki/William_Vans_Murray" title="William Vans Murray">William Vans Murray</a> on a peace mission to France. <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>, realizing that the conflict was pointless, signaled his readiness for friendly relations. At the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_1800" title="Convention of 1800">Convention of 1800</a>, the two sides agreed to return any captured ships and to allow for the peaceful transfer of non-military goods to an enemy of the nation. The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Alliance_(1778)" title="Treaty of Alliance (1778)">Treaty of Alliance of 1778</a> was superseded and the United States was then free of foreign entanglements, as Washington had advised in his farewell address. Adams brought in <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a> as Secretary of State and demobilized the emergency army.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Adams proudly avoided war, but deeply split his party in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alien_and_Sedition_Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts" title="Alien and Sedition Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width: 220px;">
<div style="width: 220px; height: 270px; overflow: hidden;">
<div style="position: relative; top: -67px; left: -52px; width: 325px"><a href="/wiki/File:ADAMS,John-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="BEP engraved portrait of Adams as President" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/ADAMS%2CJohn-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/325px-ADAMS%2CJohn-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg" width="325" height="376" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/ADAMS%2CJohn-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/488px-ADAMS%2CJohn-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/ADAMS%2CJohn-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg/650px-ADAMS%2CJohn-President_%28BEP_engraved_portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8488" data-file-height="9818" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ADAMS,John-President_(BEP_engraved_portrait).jpg" title="File:ADAMS,John-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg">File:ADAMS,John-President (BEP engraved portrait).jpg</a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Engraving_and_Printing" title="Bureau of Engraving and Printing">BEP</a> engraved portrait of Adams as President</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite the discredit of the XYZ Affair, the Democratic–Republicans' opposition persisted. In the midst of war, which included the "<a href="/wiki/Reign_of_terror" class="mw-redirect" title="Reign of terror">reign of terror</a>" during the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a>, political tensions were incendiary. Some pro-French Democratic-Republicans even fostered a movement in America, similar to the French Revolution, to overthrow the Federalists.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> When Democratic-Republicans in some states refused to enforce federal laws, some Federalists voiced the intention to send in an army and force them to capitulate. As the hostility sweeping Europe bled over into America, calls for secession began to reach new heights.<sup id="cite_ref-Knott_Alexander_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Knott_Alexander-139">[139]</a></sup> Some Federalists accused the French and their associated immigrants of provoking civil unrest. In an attempt to quell the uprising, the Federalists introduced, and the Congress passed, a series of laws collectively referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts" title="Alien and Sedition Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</a>, which were signed by Adams in 1798.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._17_122-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._17-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>Congress specifically passed four measures – the <a href="/wiki/Naturalization_Act_of_1798" title="Naturalization Act of 1798">Naturalization Act</a>, the Alien Friends Act, the Alien Enemies Act and the Sedition Act. These statutes were designed to mitigate the threat of secessionists by disallowing their most extreme firebrands. The Naturalization Act increased to 14 years the period of residence required for an immigrant to attain American citizenship (naturalized citizens tended to vote for the Democratic-Republicans.) The Alien Friends Act and the Alien Enemies Act allowed the president to deport any foreigner (from friendly and hostile nations, respectively) which he considered dangerous to the country. The Sedition Act made it a crime to publish "false, scandalous, and malicious writing" against the government or its officials. Punishments included 2–5 years in prison and fines of up to $5,000. Although Adams had not promoted any of these acts, he signed them into law.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._16_105-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._16-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>The acts became controversial from prosecution thereunder of a Congressman and a number of newspaper editors. Indeed, the Federalist administration initiated fourteen or more indictments under the Sedition Act, as well as suits against five of the six most prominent Democratic–Republican newspapers. The majority of the legal actions began in 1798 and 1799, and went to trial on the eve of the 1800 presidential election–timing that hardly appeared coincidental, according to biographer Ferling. Other historians have cited evidence that the Alien and Sedition Acts were rarely enforced, namely: 1) only 10 convictions under the Sedition Act have been identified; 2) Adams never signed a deportation order; and 3) the sources of expressed furor over the acts were Democratic–Republicans. However, other historians have emphasized that the Acts were employed for political targeting from the outset, causing many aliens to leave the country. The Acts as well allowed for prosecution of many who opposed the Federalists, even on the floor of Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChernow2004668_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChernow2004668-140">[140]</a></sup> In any case, the election of 1800 in fact became a bitter and volatile contest, with each side expressing extraordinary fear of the other and its policies; after Democratic–Republicans prevailed in the elections of 1800, they used the acts against Federalists before the laws finally expired.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._17_122-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._17-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Move_to_Washington">Move to Washington</span></h3>
<p>Adams made his first official visit to the nation's new <a href="/wiki/Seat_of_government" title="Seat of government">seat of government</a> in early June 1800. Amid the "raw and unfinished" cityscape, the president found the public buildings "in a much greater forwardness of completion than expected."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith19621036_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith19621036-141">[141]</a></sup> He moved into the nearly completed <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">President's Mansion</a> (later known as the White House) on November 1. Abigail arrived a few weeks later. Upon arriving, Adams wrote to her, "Before I end my letter, I pray Heaven to bestow the best of Blessings on this House and all that shall hereafter inhabit it. May none but honest and wise Men ever rule under this roof."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith19621049_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith19621049-142">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> The Senate of the <a href="/wiki/6th_United_States_Congress" title="6th United States Congress">Sixth Congress</a> met for the first time in the new <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">Congress House</a> (later known as the Capitol building) on November 17, 1800. Several days later, on November 22, Adams delivered his fourth <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union_Address" class="mw-redirect" title="State of the Union Address">State of the Union Address</a> to a joint session of Congress in the <a href="/wiki/Old_Supreme_Court_Chamber" title="Old Supreme Court Chamber">Senate chamber</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith19621050_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith19621050-144">[144]</a></sup> This would be the last annual message any president would personally deliver to Congress for the next 113 years.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1800">Election of 1800</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1800" title="United States presidential election, 1800">United States presidential election, 1800</a></div>
<p>With the Federalist Party was deeply split over his negotiations with France, and the opposition Democratic–Republicans enraged over the Alien and Sedition Acts, and the expansion of the military, Adams faced a daunting reelection campaign in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1800" title="United States presidential election, 1800">1800</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-JAelections_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JAelections-101">[101]</a></sup> As in 1796, the Federalist members of Congress caucused in the spring of 1800 and nominated Adams and Charles C. Pinckney. The Democratic–Republicans meanwhile nominated Jefferson and Burr, their candidates in the previous election.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._19_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._19-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>The campaign was bitter and characterized by malicious personal attacks. Federalists spread rumors that the Democratic–Republicans were radicals who would ruin the country through revolution. They in turn accused Federalists of subverting republican principles through punitive federal laws, and of favoring Britain and the other coalition countries in their war with France in order to promote aristocratic, anti-republican values. Additionally, Hamilton was hard at work, attempting to sabotage the president's reelection. In October, he published a pamphlet in which he charged that Adams was "emotionally unstable, given to impulsive and irrational decisions, unable to coexist with his closest advisers, and generally unfit to be president." He also worked at persuading Federalist presidential electors in <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> to withhold their vote from Adams, hoping to boosting Pinckney's chances of winning the presidency.<sup id="cite_ref-JAelections_101-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JAelections-101">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._19_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._19-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable sortable floatright" style="margin-left: 1em">

<td style="background-color:#F6D6C9"></td>

</tr>
</table>
<p>When the electoral votes were counted, Adams finished in third place with 65 votes, and Pinckney came in fourth with 64 votes (one New England Federalist elector voted for John Jay instead). Jefferson and Burr tied for first place with 73 votes each. Because of the tie, the election devolved upon the House of Representatives. As specified by the Constitution, each state's delegation voted <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/en_bloc" class="extiw" title="wikt:en bloc">en bloc</a></i>, with each state having a single vote; an <a href="/wiki/Supermajority#Majority_of_the_entire_membership" title="Supermajority">absolute majority</a> (nine, as there were 16 states at the time) was required for victory. On February 17, 1801—following 36th ballot—Jefferson was elected by a vote of 10 to 4 (two states abstained).<sup id="cite_ref-JAelections_101-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JAelections-101">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ECscore_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ECscore-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historian <a href="/wiki/John_E._Ferling" title="John E. Ferling">John E. Ferling</a> attributes Adams' defeat to five factors: the stronger organization of the Democratic–Republicans; Federalist disunity; the controversy surrounding the Alien and Sedition Acts; the popularity of Jefferson in the south; and, the effective politicking of Aaron Burr in <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, where the State Legislature shifted from Federalist to Democratic–Republican on the basis of a few wards in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> controlled by Burr's political machine.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._19_146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._19-146">[146]</a></sup> Analyzing the causes of the party's trouncing, Adams wrote, "No party that ever existed knew itself so little or so vainly overrated its own influence and popularity as ours. None ever understood so ill the causes of its own power, or so wantonly destroyed them."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith19621053_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith19621053-147">[147]</a></sup></p>
<p>To compound the agony of his defeat, Adams' son Charles, a long-time alcoholic, died in late November. Anxious to rejoin Abigail, who had already left for Massachusetts, Adams departed the White House in the predawn hours of March 4, 1801, and did not attend <a href="/wiki/First_inauguration_of_Thomas_Jefferson" title="First inauguration of Thomas Jefferson">Jefferson's inauguration</a>. Since him, only three out-going presidents (having served a full term) have not attended their successor's inauguration.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._19_146-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._19-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>The complications arising out of the 1796 and 1800 elections prompted Congress and the states to refine the process whereby the Electoral College elects a president and a vice president. The new procedure was enacted through the <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twelfth Amendment to the United States Constitution">12th Amendment</a>, which became a part of the Constitution in June 1804, and was first followed in that year's <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1804" title="United States presidential election, 1804">presidential election</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_federal_judges_appointed_by_John_Adams" title="List of federal judges appointed by John Adams">List of federal judges appointed by John Adams</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman,_1832.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman%2C_1832.jpg/220px-John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman%2C_1832.jpg" width="220" height="273" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman%2C_1832.jpg/330px-John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman%2C_1832.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman%2C_1832.jpg/440px-John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman%2C_1832.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1059" data-file-height="1312" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Marshall_by_Henry_Inman,_1832.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John Marshall, 4th Chief Justice of the U.S. Supreme Court</div>
</div>
</div>
<table class="infobox" cellspacing="5" style="width: auto; text-align:left; line-height: 1.2em; margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 1em; float: left; clear: left;">
<tr>
<th colspan="3" style="line-height: 1.5em; font-size: 110%; background:#DCDCDC; text-align: center;">Supreme Court Appointments by President Adams</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="col">Position</th>
<th scope="col">Name</th>
<th scope="col">Term</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" style="background: #000"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td rowspan="1"><a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a></td>
<th style=""><a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a></th>
<td>1801–1835</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" style="background: #000"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Associate_Justice_of_the_Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Associate Justice of the Supreme Court of the United States">Associate Justice</a></td>
<th style=""><a href="/wiki/Bushrod_Washington" title="Bushrod Washington">Bushrod Washington</a></th>
<td>1799–1829</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th style=""><a href="/wiki/Alfred_Moore" title="Alfred Moore">Alfred Moore</a></th>
<td>1800–1804</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Adams appointed two U.S. Supreme Court associate justices during his term in office: Bushrod Washington, the nephew of American founding father and President George Washington, to succeed <a href="/wiki/James_Wilson" title="James Wilson">James Wilson</a>; and Alfred Moore, who succeeded <a href="/wiki/James_Iredell" title="James Iredell">James Iredell</a>. He also named John Marshall as the fourth Chief Justice of the United States, replacing <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Ellsworth" title="Oliver Ellsworth">Oliver Ellsworth</a>, who had retired due to ill health. Marshall's long tenure represents the most lasting influence of the Federalists, as he infused the Constitution with a judicious and carefully reasoned nationalistic interpretation and established the Judicial Branch as the equal of the Executive and Legislative branches.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._19_146-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._19-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the Federalists lost control of both houses of Congress along with the White House in the election of 1800, the <a href="/wiki/Lame-duck_session" title="Lame-duck session">lame-duck session</a> of the <a href="/wiki/6th_United_States_Congress" title="6th United States Congress">6th Congress</a> in February 1801 approved a judiciary act, commonly known as the <a href="/wiki/Midnight_Judges_Act" title="Midnight Judges Act">Midnight Judges Act</a>, which created a set of federal appeals courts between the district courts and the Supreme Court. Adams filled the vacancies created in this statute by appointing a series of judges, whom his opponents called the "Midnight Judges" the appointments were issued just days before his presidential term expired. Most of these judges lost their posts when the <a href="/wiki/7th_United_States_Congress" title="7th United States Congress">7th Congress</a>, with a solid Democratic-Republican majority, approved the <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_Act_of_1802" title="Judiciary Act of 1802">Judiciary Act of 1802</a>, abolishing the newly created courts.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement">Retirement</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="An unsmiling elderly man sits in a red chair, slightly pointing left." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg/220px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg" width="220" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg/330px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg/440px-Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1266" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Brooklyn_Museum_-_Portrait_of_John_Adams_-_Samuel_Finley_Breese_Morse_-_overall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John Adams, ca 1816, by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_F.B._Morse" class="mw-redirect" title="Samuel F.B. Morse">Samuel F.B. Morse</a> (<a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Museum_of_Art" class="mw-redirect" title="Brooklyn Museum of Art">Brooklyn Museum</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Adams resumed farming at his home <a href="/wiki/Peacefield" title="Peacefield">Peacefield</a> in the town of Quincy; he also began work on an autobiography (which he never finished) and resumed correspondence with such old friends as <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Waterhouse" title="Benjamin Waterhouse">Benjamin Waterhouse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Rush" title="Benjamin Rush">Benjamin Rush</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling_1992_ch_20_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling_1992_ch_20-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>Aside from this, Adams generally stayed quiet. He did not publicly criticize Jefferson's actions as president,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001595_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001595-151">[151]</a></sup> believing that "instead of opposing Systematically any Administration, running down their Characters and opposing all their Measures right or wrong, We ought to Support every Administration as far as We can in Justice." He did, however, privately attack the president over his <a href="/wiki/Embargo_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Embargo Act">Embargo Act</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> despite the fact that it was voted for by his son John Quincy, a U.S. senator.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001598-599_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001598-599-153">[153]</a></sup> Another incident began in relation to <a href="/wiki/Mercy_Otis_Warren" title="Mercy Otis Warren">Mercy Otis Warren</a> in 1806. Warren, an old friend, had attacked Adams in a pamphlet for his "partiality for monarchy" and "pride of talents and much ambition." A tempestuous correspondence ensued. In time, the friendship healed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001594-596_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001594-596-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Jefferson's retirement from public life in 1809, Adams became more vocal. He published a three-year marathon of letters in the <i><a href="/wiki/Boston_Patriot_(newspaper)" title="Boston Patriot (newspaper)">Boston Patriot</a></i> newspaper, refuting line-by-line an 1800 pamphlet by Hamilton which attacked his conduct and character. Though Hamilton had died in 1804 in <a href="/wiki/Burr%E2%80%93Hamilton_duel" title="Burr–Hamilton duel">a duel with Aaron Burr</a>, Adams felt the need to vindicate his character against the New Yorker's vehement charges.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling_1992_ch_20_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling_1992_ch_20-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>The years of retirement in the Adams' household were not without some temporary financial adversity; in 1803 the bank holding his cash reserves of about $13,000 collapsed. John Quincy came to the rescue by purchasing from him his properties in Weymouth and Quincy, including Peacefield, for the sum of $12,800.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling_1992_ch_20_150-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling_1992_ch_20-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>Daughter <a href="/wiki/Abigail_Adams_Smith" title="Abigail Adams Smith">Abigail ("Nabby")</a> was married to <a href="/wiki/Congressman" class="mw-redirect" title="Congressman">Representative</a> <a href="/wiki/William_Stephens_Smith" title="William Stephens Smith">William Stephens Smith</a>, but she returned to her parents' home after the failure of the marriage; she died of breast cancer in 1813. His wife Abigail died of <a href="/wiki/Typhoid" class="mw-redirect" title="Typhoid">typhoid</a> on October 28, 1818. His son Thomas and wife Ann, along with seven children, lived with Adams to the end of Adams' life, as well as Louisa Smith (Abigail's niece by her brother William).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling_1992_ch_20_150-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling_1992_ch_20-150">[150]</a></sup> Sixteen months before John Adams' death, John Quincy became the sixth president of the United States in 1825, the only son to succeed his father as President until <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Correspondence_with_Jefferson">Correspondence with Jefferson</span></h3>
<p>In early 1801, Adams had sent Thomas Jefferson a brief note after returning to Quincy to which Jefferson did not respond. In 1804, Abigail wrote to Jefferson to express her condolences upon the death of his daughter <a href="/wiki/Mary_Jefferson_Eppes" title="Mary Jefferson Eppes">Polly</a>, who had stayed with the Adamses is London in 1787. This initiated a brief correspondence which quickly descended into political rancor. Jefferson terminated it by not replying to Abigail's fourth letter. Aside from that, there had been no communication between Peacefield and Monticello since Adams left office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling1992431_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling1992431-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early 1812, Adams reconciled with Jefferson. Their mutual friend Benjamin Rush, a fellow signer of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> who had been corresponding with both, encouraged them to reach out to each other. On New Year's Day Adams sent a brief, friendly note to Jefferson to accompany the delivery of "two pieces of homespun," a two-volume collection of lectures on rhetoric by John Quincy Adams. Jefferson replied immediately with a cordial letter, and the two men revived their friendship, which they sustained by mail. The correspondence that they resumed in 1812 lasted the rest of their lives, and has been hailed as among their great legacies of American literature.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling_1992_ch_20_150-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling_1992_ch_20-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>Their letters represent an insight into both the period and the minds of the two revolutionary leaders and presidents. The missives lasted fourteen years, and consisted of 158 letters–109 from Adams and 49 from Jefferson.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerling1992431_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerling1992431-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early on, Adams repeatedly tried to turn the correspondence to a discussion of their actions in the political arena.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001607_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001607-157">[157]</a></sup> Jefferson refused to oblige him, saying that "nothing new can be added by you or me to what has been said by others and will be said in every age."<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Adams made one more attempt, writing that "You and I ought not to die before we have explained ourselves to each other.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Still, Jefferson declined to engage Adams in this sort of discussion. Adams accepted this, and the correspondence turned to other matters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001608_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001608-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>The two men discussed "natural aristocracy." Jefferson said, "The natural aristocracy I consider as the most precious gift of nature for the instruction, the trusts, and government of society. And indeed it would have been inconsistent in creation to have formed man for the social state, and not to have provided virtue and wisdom enough to manage the concerns of society. May we not even say that the form of government is best which provides most effectually for a pure selection of these natural [aristocrats] into the offices of government?"<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> Adams wondered if it ever would be so clear who these people were, "Your distinction between natural and artificial aristocracy does not appear to me well founded. Birth and wealth are conferred on some men as imperiously by nature, as genius, strength, or beauty. ... When aristocracies are established by human laws and honour, wealth, and power are made hereditary by municipal laws and political institutions, then I acknowledge artificial aristocracy to commence." It would always be true, Adams argued, that fate would bestow influence on some men for reasons other than true wisdom and virtue. That being the way of nature, he thought such "talents" were natural. A good government, therefore, had to account for that reality.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the two grew older, the letters became fewer and farther between. There was also a great deal that they kept to themselves. Jefferson said nothing about his construction of a new house, domestic turmoil, slave ownership, or poor financial situation, while Adams did not mention the troublesome behavior of his son Thomas, who had failed as a lawyer and become an alcoholic, resorting afterwards to living primarily as a caretaker at Peacefield.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001634_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001634-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Graves_of_the_Adams,_Quincy,_Massachusetts.JPG" class="image"><img alt="3 marble sarcophagi, one in the foreground, 2 in the background are seen. 2 are seen with flags of the United States at the top." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG/220px-Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG/330px-Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG/440px-Graves_of_the_Adams%2C_Quincy%2C_Massachusetts.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Graves_of_the_Adams,_Quincy,_Massachusetts.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Tombs of John Adams (far) and John Quincy Adams (near), in family crypt at <a href="/wiki/United_First_Parish_Church" title="United First Parish Church">United First Parish Church</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Less than a month before his death, Adams issued a statement about the destiny of the United States, which historians such as <a href="/wiki/Joy_Hakim" title="Joy Hakim">Joy Hakim</a> have characterized as a "warning" for his fellow citizens: "My best wishes, in the joys, and festivities, and the solemn services of that day on which will be completed the fiftieth year from its birth, of the independence of the United States: a memorable epoch in the annals of the human race, destined in future history to form the brightest or the blackest page, according to the use or the abuse of those political institutions by which they shall, in time to come, be shaped by the human mind."<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 4, 1826, the fiftieth anniversary of the adoption of the Declaration of Independence, Adams died at his home in Quincy, at approximately 6:20 PM.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001622_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001622-165">[165]</a></sup> Adams' crypt lies at <a href="/wiki/United_First_Parish_Church" title="United First Parish Church">United First Parish Church</a> in Quincy, Massachusetts, with his wife Abigail and son John Quincy Adams.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> When Adams died, his last words included an acknowledgement of his longtime friend and rival: "Thomas Jefferson survives." Adams was, in fact, unaware that Jefferson had died several hours before.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001646_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001646-167">[167]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEllis2003248_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEllis2003248-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_philosophy_and_views">Political philosophy and views</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery">Slavery</span></h3>
<p>Adams never bought a slave and declined on principle to utilize slave labor, saying, "I have, through my whole life, held the practice of slavery in such abhorrence, that I have never owned a negro or any other slave, though I have lived for many years in times, when the practice was not disgraceful, when the best men in my vicinity thought it not inconsistent with their character, and when it has cost me thousands of dollars for the labor and subsistence of free men, which I might have saved by the purchase of negroes at times when they were very cheap."<sup id="cite_ref-RJEvans_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RJEvans-169">[169]</a></sup> Adams generally tried to keep the issue out of national politics, because of the anticipated southern response during a time when unity was needed to achieve independence.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiencek_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiencek-170">[170]</a></sup> He spoke out in 1777 against a bill to emancipate slaves in Massachusetts, saying that the issue was presently too divisive, and so the legislation should "sleep for a time."<sup id="cite_ref-Wiencek_170-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiencek-170">[170]</a></sup> He also was against use of black soldiers in the Revolution, due to opposition from southerners.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiencek_170-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiencek-170">[170]</a></sup> Slavery was abolished in Massachusetts about 1780, when it was forbidden by implication in the Declaration of Rights that John Adams wrote into the Massachusetts Constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> Abigail Adams, on the other hand, vocally opposed slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._1_4-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._1-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accusations_of_monarchism">Accusations of monarchism</span></h3>
<p>Throughout his lifetime Adams expressed controversial and shifting views regarding the virtues of monarchical and hereditary political institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-Mark_O._Hatfield.2C_Vice_Pres_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mark_O._Hatfield.2C_Vice_Pres-172">[172]</a></sup> At times he conveyed substantial support for these approaches,<sup id="cite_ref-Old_Family_Letters_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Old_Family_Letters-173">[173]</a></sup> suggesting for example that "hereditary monarchy or aristocracy" are the "only institutions that can possibly preserve the laws and liberties of the people."<sup id="cite_ref-Old_Family_Letters_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Old_Family_Letters-173">[173]</a></sup> Yet at other times he distanced himself from such ideas, calling himself "a mortal and irreconcilable enemy to Monarchy" and "no friend to hereditary limited monarchy in America."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001410_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough2001410-174">[174]</a></sup> Such denials did not assuage his critics, and Adams was often accused of being a monarchist.<sup id="cite_ref-John_.26_Abigail_Adams_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_.26_Abigail_Adams-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg" class="image"><img alt="An elderly man sits in a red chair with his arms crossed, looking slightly left." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg/220px-Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg" width="220" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg/330px-Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg/440px-Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="853" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_Stuart_John_Adams.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John Adams by <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Stuart" title="Gilbert Stuart">Gilbert Stuart</a> (1823). This portrait was the last ever made of Adams, done at the request of John Quincy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Many of these attacks are considered to have been scurrilous, including suggestions that he was planning to "crown himself king" and "grooming John Quincy as heir to the throne".<sup id="cite_ref-John_.26_Abigail_Adams_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_.26_Abigail_Adams-175">[175]</a></sup> However, Peter Shaw has argued that: "[T]he inevitable attacks on Adams, crude as they were, stumbled on a truth that he did not admit to himself. He was leaning toward monarchy and aristocracy (as distinct from kings and aristocrats) at the time he wrote 'Davila', though he did not directly reveal this in its essays. Decidedly, sometime after he became vice-president, Adams concluded that the United States would have to adopt a hereditary legislature and a monarch... and he outlined a plan by which state conventions would appoint hereditary senators while a national one appointed a president for life."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShaw1975230-237_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShaw1975230-237-176">[176]</a></sup> In contradiction to such notions, Adams asserted in a letter to Thomas Jefferson: "If you suppose that I have ever had a design or desire of attempting to introduce a government of King, Lords and Commons, or in other words an hereditary Executive, or an hereditary Senate, either into the government of the United States, or that of any individual state, in this country, you are wholly mistaken. There is not such a thought expressed or intimated in any public writing or private letter of mine, and I may safely challenge all of mankind to produce such a passage and quote the chapter and verse."<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_views">Religious views</span></h3>
<p>Adams was raised a <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregationalist</a>, since his ancestors were Puritans. According to biographer <a href="/wiki/David_McCullough" title="David McCullough">David McCullough</a>, "as his family and friends knew, Adams was both a devout Christian, and an independent thinker".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough200118_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough200118-178">[178]</a></sup> In a letter to Benjamin Rush, Adams credited religion with the success of his ancestors since their migration to the New World in the 1630s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCullough200122_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCullough200122-179">[179]</a></sup> Adams was educated at Harvard when the influence of <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a> was growing there, and sometimes used deistic terms in his speeches and writing.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling_1992_ch_20_150-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling_1992_ch_20-150">[150]</a></sup> He also believed that regular church service was beneficial to man's moral sense. Everett (1966) concludes that "Adams strove for a religion based on a common sense sort of reasonableness" and maintained that religion must change and evolve toward perfection.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> Fielding (1940) argues that Adams' beliefs synthesized Puritan, deist, and <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanist</a> concepts. Adams at one point said that Christianity had originally been <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelatory</a>, but was being misinterpreted and misused in the service of superstition, fraud, and unscrupulous power.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> Goff (1993) acknowledges Fielding's "persuasive argument that Adams never was a deist because he allowed the suspension of the laws of nature and believed that evil was internal, not the result of external institutions."<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup></p>
<p>Frazer (2004) notes that while Adams shared many perspectives with deists, "Adams clearly was not a deist. Deism rejected any and all supernatural activity and intervention by God; consequently, deists did not believe in miracles or God's providence....Adams, however, did believe in miracles, providence, and, to a certain extent, the Bible as revelation."<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> Frazer further argues that Adams' "theistic rationalism, like that of the other Founders, was a sort of middle ground between Protestantism and deism."<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> By contrast, David L. Holmes has argued that Adams, beginning as a Congregationalist, ended his days as a Christian Unitarian, accepting central tenets of the Unitarian creed, but also accepting Jesus as the redeemer of humanity and the biblical account of his miracles as true.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolmes200673.E2.80.9378_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolmes200673.E2.80.9378-185">[185]</a></sup> Like many of his Protestant contemporaries, Adams criticized the claims to universal authority made by the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_ch._16_105-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_ch._16-105">[105]</a></sup> In 1796, Adams denounced political opponent <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a>'s deistic criticisms of Christianity in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Reason" title="The Age of Reason">The Age of Reason</a></i>, saying, "The Christian religion is, above all the religions that ever prevailed or existed in ancient or modern times, the religion of wisdom, virtue, equity and humanity, let the Blackguard Paine say what he will."<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" width="16" height="16" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.c-span.org/video/?166023-10/john-adams">Presentation by McCullough on <i>John Adams</i> at the National Book Festival, September 8, 2001</a>, <a href="/wiki/C-SPAN" title="C-SPAN">C-SPAN</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Adams' grandson <a href="/wiki/Charles_Francis_Adams_Sr." title="Charles Francis Adams Sr.">Charles Francis Adams Sr.</a> edited the first two volumes of <i>The Works of John Adams, Esq., Second President of the United States</i>. These were published between 1850 and 1856 by Charles C. Little and James Brown in Boston. The first seven chapters were produced by John Quincy Adams.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_Select_Bibliography_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_Select_Bibliography-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>The premier modern biography was <i>Honest John Adams</i>, a 1933 biography by the noted French specialist in American history Gilbert Chinard, who came to Adams after writing his acclaimed 1929 biography of Jefferson. For a generation, Chinard's work was regarded as the best life of Adams, and it is still an important text in illustrating the themes of Adams' biographical and historical scholarship. Following the opening of the Adams family papers in the 1950s, <a href="/wiki/Page_Smith" title="Page Smith">Page Smith</a> published the first major biography to use these previously inaccessible primary sources; his biography won a 1962 <a href="/wiki/Bancroft_Prize" title="Bancroft Prize">Bancroft Prize</a> but was criticized for its scanting of Adams' intellectual life and its diffuseness. In 1975, Peter Shaw published <i>The Character of John Adams,</i> a thematic biography noted for its psychological insight into Adams' life. The 1992 character study by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_J._Ellis" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph J. Ellis">Joseph J. Ellis</a>, <i>Passionate Sage: The Character and Legacy of John Adams,</i> was Ellis's first major publishing success and remains one of the most useful and insightful studies of Adams' personality. In 1992, the Revolutionary War historian and biographer <a href="/wiki/John_E._Ferling" title="John E. Ferling">John E. Ferling</a> published his acclaimed <i>John Adams: A Life</i>, also noted for its psychological sensitivity.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferling.2C_Select_Bibliography_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferling.2C_Select_Bibliography-187">[187]</a></sup> David McCullough authored the 2001 biography <i><a href="/wiki/John_Adams_(book)" title="John Adams (book)">John Adams</a></i>, which won various awards and was the basis for a 2008 <a href="/wiki/John_Adams_(miniseries)" title="John Adams (miniseries)">TV miniseries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup></p>

<td style="width: 30%; text-align: center;" rowspan="2"><b>New political party</b></td>
<td style="width: 40%; text-align: center;" rowspan="1"><b><a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist</a> nominee for<br />
Vice President of the United States</b><br />
<a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1792" title="United States presidential election, 1792">1792</a>¹²</td>
<td style="width: 30%; text-align: center;" rowspan="1">Succeeded&#160;by<br />
<span style="font-weight: bold"><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Pinckney" title="Thomas Pinckney">Thomas Pinckney</a>²</span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="width: 40%; text-align: center;" rowspan="1"><b><a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist</a> nominee for<br />
President of the United States</b><br />
<a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1796" title="United States presidential election, 1796">1796</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1800" title="United States presidential election, 1800">1800</a></td>
<td style="width: 30%; text-align: center;" rowspan="1">Succeeded&#160;by<br />
<span style="font-weight: bold"><a href="/wiki/Charles_Cotesworth_Pinckney" title="Charles Cotesworth Pinckney">Charles Cotesworth Pinckney</a></span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th colspan="3" style="border-top: 5px solid #FFF157;">Honorary titles</th>
</tr>
<tr style="text-align:center;">
<td style="width:30%;" rowspan="1">Preceded&#160;by<br />
<span style="font-weight: bold"><a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a></span></td>
<td style="width: 40%; text-align: center;" rowspan="1"><b><a href="/wiki/Oldest_living_President_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Oldest living President of the United States">Oldest living President of the United States</a></b><br />
1799–1826</td>
<td style="width: 30%; text-align: center;" rowspan="1">Succeeded&#160;by<br />
<span style="font-weight: bold"><a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a></span></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" style="border-top: 5px solid #bebebe;"><small><b>Notes and references</b></small></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" style="text-align:left;"><small>1. While Adams won the Vice Presidency in <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1789" class="mw-redirect" title="United States presidential election, 1789">1789</a> as well, he was not the candidate of the Federalist Party, which had not yet formed.</small><br />
<small>2. Technically, Adams was a presidential candidate in 1792 and Pinckney was a presidential candidate in 1796. Prior to the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twelfth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Twelfth Amendment</a> in 1804, each presidential elector could cast two ballots; the highest vote-getter would become President and the runner-up would become Vice President. Thus, in 1792, with <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> as the prohibitive favorite for President, the Federalist party fielded Adams as a presidential candidate, with the intention that he be elected to the Vice Presidency. Similarly, in 1796 and 1800, the Federalist party fielded two candidates, Adams and Thomas Pinckney in 1796 and Adams and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Cotesworth_Pinckney" title="Charles Cotesworth Pinckney">Charles Pinckney</a> in 1800, with the intention that Adams be elected President and Pinckney be elected Vice President.</small></td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</div>
